<p><b>Hong Kong</b> (<span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt" lang="en-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'h' in 'hi'">h</span><span title="/ɒ/: 'o' in 'body'">ɒ</span><span title="/ŋ/: 'ng' in 'sing'">ŋ</span><span title="'k' in 'kind'">k</span><span title="/ɒ/: 'o' in 'body'">ɒ</span><span title="/ŋ/: 'ng' in 'sing'">ŋ</span></span>/</a></span></span> (US) or <span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt" lang="en-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="'h' in 'hi'">h</span><span title="/ɒ/: 'o' in 'body'">ɒ</span><span title="/ŋ/: 'ng' in 'sing'">ŋ</span><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'k' in 'kind'">k</span><span title="/ɒ/: 'o' in 'body'">ɒ</span><span title="/ŋ/: 'ng' in 'sing'">ŋ</span></span>/</a></span></span> (UK); <a href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese_characters" title="Traditional Chinese characters">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh-Hant">香港</span>, <span style="font-size:85%;"><a href="/wiki/Hong_Kong_Cantonese" title="Hong Kong Cantonese">Cantonese</a>:</span> <small></small><span class="IPA" lang="yue-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Cantonese" title="Help:IPA/Cantonese">[hœ́ːŋ.kɔ̌ːŋ]</a></span> <span class="nowrap" style="font-size:85%">(<span class="unicode haudio"><span class="fn"><span style="white-space:nowrap;margin-right:.25em;"><a href="/wiki/File:Yue-heung1gong2.ogg" title="About this sound"><img alt="" data-file-height="20" data-file-width="20" decoding="async" height="11" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/11px-Loudspeaker.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/17px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/22px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 2x" width="11"/></a></span><a class="internal" href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/5e/Yue-heung1gong2.ogg" title="Yue-heung1gong2.ogg">listen</a></span></span>)</span>), officially the <b>Hong Kong Special Administrative Region of the People's Republic of China</b> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abbr." title="Abbr.">abbr.</a> <b>Hong Kong SAR</b> or <b>HKSAR</b>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[d]</a></sup> is a <a href="/wiki/List_of_cities_in_China" title="List of cities in China">city</a> and <a href="/wiki/Special_administrative_regions_of_China" title="Special administrative regions of China">special administrative region</a> of <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a> on the eastern <a href="/wiki/Pearl_River_Delta" title="Pearl River Delta">Pearl River Delta</a> in <a href="/wiki/South_China" title="South China">South China</a>. With 7.5 million residents of various nationalities<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[e]</a></sup> in a 1,104-square-kilometre (426 sq mi) territory, Hong Kong is one of the <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_and_dependencies_by_population_density" title="List of countries and dependencies by population density">most densely populated places</a> in the world. Hong Kong is also a major global <a href="/wiki/Financial_centre" title="Financial centre">financial centre</a> and one of the <a href="/wiki/Global_city" title="Global city">most developed cities in the world</a>.
</p><p>Hong Kong was established as a <a href="/wiki/British_Hong_Kong" title="British Hong Kong">colony of the British Empire</a> after the <a href="/wiki/Qing_dynasty" title="Qing dynasty">Qing Empire</a> ceded <a href="/wiki/Hong_Kong_Island" title="Hong Kong Island">Hong Kong Island</a> from <a href="/wiki/Bao%27an_County" title="Bao'an County">Xin'an County</a> at the end of the <a href="/wiki/First_Opium_War" title="First Opium War">First Opium War</a> in 1841 then again in 1842.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CarrollEarlyHistory_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-CarrollEarlyHistory-23">[18]</a></sup> The colony expanded to the <a href="/wiki/Kowloon_Peninsula" title="Kowloon Peninsula">Kowloon Peninsula</a> in 1860 after the <a href="/wiki/Second_Opium_War" title="Second Opium War">Second Opium War</a> and was further extended when Britain obtained a <a href="/wiki/99-year_lease" title="99-year lease">99-year lease</a> of the <a href="/wiki/New_Territories" title="New Territories">New Territories</a> in 1898.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SecondOpiumWar_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-SecondOpiumWar-24">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NTLease_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-NTLease-25">[20]</a></sup> British Hong Kong was <a href="/wiki/Japanese_occupation_of_Hong_Kong" title="Japanese occupation of Hong Kong">occupied</a> by <a href="/wiki/Empire_of_Japan" title="Empire of Japan">Imperial Japan</a> from <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Hong_Kong" title="Battle of Hong Kong">1941</a> to <a href="/wiki/Liberation_Day_(Hong_Kong)" title="Liberation Day (Hong Kong)">1945</a> during <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>; British administration resumed after the <a href="/wiki/Surrender_of_Japan" title="Surrender of Japan">surrender of Japan</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-snow_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-snow-26">[21]</a></sup> The whole territory was <a href="/wiki/Handover_of_Hong_Kong" title="Handover of Hong Kong">transferred to China</a> in 1997.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYTHandover_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYTHandover-27">[22]</a></sup> As one of China's two special administrative regions (the other being <a href="/wiki/Macau" title="Macau">Macau</a>), Hong Kong maintains separate governing and economic systems from that of <a href="/wiki/Mainland_China" title="Mainland China">mainland China</a> under the principle of "<a href="/wiki/One_country,_two_systems" title="One country, two systems">one country, two systems</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[f]</a></sup>
</p><p>Originally a sparsely populated area of farming and fishing villages,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CarrollEarlyHistory_23-1"><a href="#cite_note-CarrollEarlyHistory-23">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERen2010221_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERen2010221-30">[24]</a></sup> the territory has become one of the world's most significant financial centres and commercial ports.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[25]</a></sup> It is the world's tenth-largest exporter and ninth-largest importer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CIAExports_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-CIAExports-32">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CIAImports_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-CIAImports-33">[27]</a></sup> Hong Kong has a major <a href="/wiki/Capitalism" title="Capitalism">capitalist</a> <a href="/wiki/Service_economy" title="Service economy">service economy</a> characterised by low taxation and <a href="/wiki/Free_trade" title="Free trade">free trade</a>, and its currency, the <a href="/wiki/Hong_Kong_dollar" title="Hong Kong dollar">Hong Kong dollar</a>, is the eighth most traded currency in the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[28]</a></sup> Hong Kong is home to the <a href="/wiki/List_of_cities_by_number_of_billionaires" title="List of cities by number of billionaires">third-highest number of billionaires</a> of any city in the world,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[29]</a></sup> the second-highest number of billionaires of any city in Asia, and the largest concentration of <a href="/wiki/Ultra_high-net-worth_individual" title="Ultra high-net-worth individual">ultra high-net-worth individuals</a> of any city in the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[31]</a></sup> Although the city has one of the highest <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_GDP_(PPP)_per_capita" title="List of countries by GDP (PPP) per capita">per capita incomes</a> in the world, severe <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_income_equality" title="List of countries by income equality">income inequality</a> exists among the population.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[32]</a></sup> Most notably, <a href="/wiki/Housing_in_Hong_Kong" title="Housing in Hong Kong">housing in Hong Kong</a> has been well-documented to experience a chronic persistent shortage; the extremely compact house sizes and the extremely high <a href="/wiki/Urban_density" title="Urban density">housing density</a> are the effects of Hong Kong’s housing market being least affordable and most expensive housing market in the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[34]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[35]</a></sup>
</p><p>Hong Kong is a <a href="/wiki/Developed_country" title="Developed country">highly developed territory</a> and ranks fourth on the <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_Human_Development_Index" title="List of countries by Human Development Index">UN Human Development Index</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cia_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-cia-42">[36]</a></sup> The city has <a href="/wiki/List_of_cities_with_the_most_skyscrapers" title="List of cities with the most skyscrapers">the largest number of skyscrapers</a> of any city in the world,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SkyscraperCenter_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-SkyscraperCenter-43">[37]</a></sup> and its residents have some of the <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_life_expectancy#List_by_the_United_Nations,_for_2010–2015" title="List of countries by life expectancy">highest life expectancies</a> in the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cia_42-1"><a href="#cite_note-cia-42">[36]</a></sup> The dense space has led to a highly developed transportation network with public transport rates exceeding 90%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pubtransport_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-pubtransport-44">[38]</a></sup> Hong Kong is ranked 3rd in the <a href="/wiki/Global_Financial_Centres_Index" title="Global Financial Centres Index">Global Financial Centres Index</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GFCI_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-GFCI-45">[39]</a></sup>
</p>

href="/wiki/Bopomofo" title="Bopomofo">Bopomofo</a></th><td class="infobox-data"><span title="Chinese-language romanization"><span lang="zh-Latn" style="font-style: normal">ㄒㄧㄤ   ㄍㄤˇ<br/>ㄊㄜˋ   ㄅㄧㄝˊ<br/>ㄒㄧㄥˊ   ㄓㄥˋ   ㄑㄩ<br/>(ㄒㄧㄤ   ㄍㄤˇ   ㄊㄜˋ   ㄑㄩ)</span></span></td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row" style="font-weight:normal;"><a href="/wiki/Spelling_in_Gwoyeu_Romatzyh" title="Spelling in Gwoyeu Romatzyh">Gwoyeu Romatzyh</a></th><td class="infobox-data"><span title="Chinese-language romanization"><span lang="zh-Latn" style="font-style: normal">Shianggaang Tehbye Shyngjenqchiu<br/>(Shianggaang Tehchiu)</span></span></td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row" style="font-weight:normal;"><a href="/wiki/Wade%E2%80%93Giles" title="Wade–Giles">Wade–Giles</a></th><td class="infobox-data"><span title="Chinese-language romanization"><span lang="zh-Latn" style="font-style: normal">Hsiang<sup>1</sup>-kang<sup>3</sup> Tʻê<sup>4</sup>-⁠pieh<sup>2</sup> Hsing<sup>2</sup>-⁠chêng<sup>4</sup>-⁠chʻü<sup>1</sup><br/> (Hsiang<sup>1</sup>-kang<sup>3</sup> Tʻê<sup>4</sup>-chʻü<sup>1</sup>)</span></span></td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row" style="font-weight:normal;"><a href="/wiki/Yale_romanization_of_Mandarin" title="Yale romanization of Mandarin">Yale Romanization</a></th><td class="infobox-data"><span title="Chinese-language romanization"><span lang="zh-Latn" style="font-style: normal">Syāngggǎng Tèbyé Syíngjèngchyū<br/>(Syānggǎng Tèchyū)</span></span></td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row" style="font-weight:normal;"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Mandarin" title="Help:IPA/Mandarin">IPA</a></th><td href="/wiki/Wu_Chinese" title="Wu Chinese">Wu</a></th></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row" style="font-weight:normal;"><a href="/wiki/Romanization_of_Wu_Chinese" title="Romanization of Wu Chinese">Romanization</a></th><td class="infobox-data"><span title="Wu Chinese-language romanization"><span lang="wuu-Latn" style="font-style: normal">shian<sup>平</sup>kaon<sup>上</sup> deh<sup>入</sup>⁠bih<sup>入</sup><br/>ghan<sup>平</sup>⁠tsen<sup>去</sup>⁠chiu<sup>平</sup><br/>(shian<sup>平</sup>kaon<sup>上</sup> deh<sup>入</sup>chiu<sup>平</sup>)</span></span></td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-header" colspan="2" style="background-color: #dcffc9;"><a href="/wiki/Hakka_Chinese" title="Hakka Chinese">Hakka</a></th></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row" Tet<sup>6</sup>ki<sup>1</sup>)</span></span></td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-header" colspan="2" style="background-color: #dcffc9;"><a href="/wiki/Cantonese" title="Cantonese">Yue: Cantonese</a></th></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row" style="font-weight:normal;"><a href="/wiki/Yale_romanization_of_Cantonese" title="Yale romanization of Cantonese">Yale Romanization</a></th><td class="infobox-data"><span title="Yue Chinese-language romanization"><span lang="yue-Latn" style="font-style: normal">Hēunggóng Dahkbiht Hàhngjingkēui<br/>(Hēunggóng Dahkkēui)<br/><i>or</i><br/>Hèunggóng Dahkbiht Hàhngjingkēui<br/>(Hèunggóng Dahkkēui)</span></span></td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row" style="font-weight:normal;"><a href="/wiki/Jyutping" title="Jyutping">Jyutping</a></th><td class="infobox-data"><span title="Yue Chinese-language romanization"><span lang="yue-Latn" style="font-style: normal"><span>Hoeng<sup>1</sup>gong<sup>2</sup> Dak<sup>6</sup>bit<sup>6</sup> Hang<sup>4</sup>zing<sup>3</sup>keoi<sup>1</sup></span><br/>(<span>Hoeng<sup>1</sup>gong<sup>2</sup> Dak<sup>6</sup>keoi<sup>1</sup></span>)</span></span></td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row" style="font-weight:normal;"><a href="/wiki/Guangdong_Romanization" title="Guangdong Romanization">Canton Romanization</a></th><td class="infobox-data"><span title="Yue Chinese-language romanization"><span lang="yue-Latn" style="font-style: normal">Hêng<sup>1</sup>gong<sup>2</sup> Deg<sup>6</sup>⁠bid<sup>6</sup> Heng<sup>4</sup>⁠jing<sup>3</sup>⁠kêu<sup>1</sup><br/> (Hêng<sup>1</sup>gong<sup>2</sup> Deg<sup>6</sup>kêu<sup>1</sup>)</span></span></td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row" style="font-weight:normal;"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Cantonese" title="Help:IPA/Cantonese">IPA</a></th><td class="infobox-data"><span title="Yue Chinese-language romanization"><span lang="yue-Latn" style="font-style: normal"><small></small><span class="IPA" lang="yue-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Cantonese" title="Help:IPA/Cantonese">[hœ́ːŋ.kɔ̌ːŋ tɐ̀k̚.pìːt̚ hɐ̏ŋ.tsēŋ.kʰɵ́y]</a></span><br/>(<small></small><span class="IPA" lang="yue-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Cantonese" title="Help:IPA/Cantonese">[hœ́ːŋ.kɔ̌ːŋ tɐ̀k̚.kʰɵ́y]</a></span>)<br/><i>or</i><br/><small></small><span class="IPA" lang="yue-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Cantonese" title="Help:IPA/Cantonese">[hœ̂ːŋ.kɔ̌ːŋ tɐ̀k̚.pìːt̚ hɐ̏ŋ.tsēŋ.kʰɵ́y]</a></span><br/>(<small></small><span class="IPA" lang="yue-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Cantonese" title="Help:IPA/Cantonese">[hœ̂ːŋ.kɔ̌ːŋ tɐ̀k̚.kʰɵ́y]</a></span>)</span></span></td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-header" colspan="2" style="background-color: #dcffc9;"><a href="/wiki/Southern_Min" title="Southern Min">Southern Min</a></th></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row" style="font-weight:normal;"><a href="/wiki/Hokkien" title="Hokkien">Hokkien</a> <a href="/wiki/Pe%CC%8Dh-%C5%8De-j%C4%AB" title="Pe̍h-ōe-jī">POJ</a></th><td class="infobox-data"><span title="Min Nan Chinese-language romanization"><span lang="nan-Latn" style="font-style: normal">Hiong-kang Te̍k-⁠pia̍t Hêng-⁠chèng-⁠khu<br/>(Hiong-kang Te̍k-khu)</span></span></td></tr></tbody></table></td><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1066479718" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/></tr><tr style="display:none"><td colspan="2">
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>The name of the territory, first <a href="/wiki/Romanization" title="Romanization">romanised</a> as "He-Ong-Kong" in 1780,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[42]</a></sup> originally referred to a small inlet located between <a href="/wiki/Ap_Lei_Chau" title="Ap Lei Chau">Aberdeen Island</a> and the southern coast of Hong Kong Island. <a href="/wiki/Aberdeen,_Hong_Kong" title="Aberdeen, Hong Kong">Aberdeen</a> was an initial point of contact between British sailors and local fishermen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[43]</a></sup> Although the source of the romanised name is unknown, it is generally believed to be an early phonetic rendering of the <a href="/wiki/Cantonese" title="Cantonese">Cantonese</a> (or <a href="/wiki/Tanka_people#Dialect" title="Tanka people">Tanka Cantonese</a>) phrase <i>hēung góng</i>. The name translates as "fragrant harbour" or "incense harbour".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HerbariumName_46-1"><a href="#cite_note-HerbariumName-46">[40]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CarrollHKName_47-1"><a href="#cite_note-CarrollHKName-47">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-etym_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-etym-50">[44]</a></sup> "Fragrant" may refer to the sweet taste of the harbour's freshwater influx from the Pearl River or to the odour from incense factories lining the coast of northern <a href="/wiki/Kowloon" title="Kowloon">Kowloon</a>. The incense was stored near Aberdeen Harbour for export before <a href="/wiki/Victoria_Harbour" title="Victoria Harbour">Victoria Harbour</a> was developed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-etym_50-1"><a href="#cite_note-etym-50">[44]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/John_Francis_Davis" title="John Francis Davis">Sir John Davis</a> (the second colonial governor) offered an alternative origin; Davis said that the name derived from "Hoong-keang" ("red torrent"), reflecting the colour of soil over which a waterfall on the island flowed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[45]</a></sup>
</p><p>The simplified name <i>Hong Kong</i> was frequently used by 1810.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[46]</a></sup> The name was also commonly written as the single word <i>Hongkong</i> until 1926, when the government officially adopted the two-word name.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[47]</a></sup> Some corporations founded during the early colonial era still keep this name, including <a href="/wiki/Hongkong_Land" title="Hongkong Land">Hongkong Land</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hongkong_Electric_Company" title="Hongkong Electric Company">Hongkong Electric Company</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hongkong_and_Shanghai_Hotels" title="Hongkong and Shanghai Hotels">Hongkong and Shanghai Hotels</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Hongkong_and_Shanghai_Banking_Corporation" title="The Hongkong and Shanghai Banking Corporation">the Hongkong and Shanghai Banking Corporation</a> (HSBC).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[48]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HSBC_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-HSBC-55">[49]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Hong_Kong" title="History of Hong Kong">History of Hong Kong</a> and <a href="/wiki/History_of_China" title="History of China">History of China</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Prehistory_and_Imperial_China">Prehistory and Imperial China</span></h3>
<p>Earliest known human traces in what is now Hong Kong are dated by some to 35,000 and 39,000 years ago during the <a href="/wiki/Paleolithic" title="Paleolithic">Paleolithic</a> period. The claim is based on an archaeological investigation in <a href="/wiki/Wong_Tei_Tung" title="Wong Tei Tung">Wong Tei Tung</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sai_Kung_Peninsula" title="Sai Kung Peninsula">Sai Kung</a> in 2003. The archaeological works revealed <a href="/wiki/Knapping" title="Knapping">knapped</a> <a href="/wiki/Stone_tool" title="Stone tool">stone tools</a> from deposits that were dated using optical <a href="/wiki/Luminescence_dating" title="Luminescence dating">luminescence dating</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[50]</a></sup>
</p><p>During the <a href="/wiki/Neolithic" title="Neolithic">Middle Neolithic</a> period, about 6,000 years ago, the region had been widely occupied by humans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Meacham2_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-Meacham2-57">[51]</a></sup> Neolithic to <a href="/wiki/Bronze_Age" title="Bronze Age">Bronze Age</a> Hong Kong settlers were semi-coastal people. Early inhabitants are believed to be <a href="/wiki/Austronesian_peoples" title="Austronesian peoples">Austronesians</a> in the Middle Neolithic period and later the <a href="/wiki/Baiyue" title="Baiyue">Yueh</a> people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Meacham2_57-1"><a href="#cite_note-Meacham2-57">[51]</a></sup> As hinted by the archaeological works in Sha Ha, Sai Kung, rice cultivation had been introduced since <a href="/wiki/Late_Neolithic" title="Late Neolithic">Late Neolithic</a> period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Li38_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-Li38-58">[52]</a></sup> Bronze Age Hong Kong was featured with coarse pottery, hard pottery, quartz and stone jewelry, as well as small bronze implements.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Meacham2_57-2"><a href="#cite_note-Meacham2-57">[51]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Qin_dynasty" title="Qin dynasty">Qin dynasty</a> incorporated the Hong Kong area into China for the first time in 214 BCE, after <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Qin_campaign_against_the_Yue_tribes" title="Qin campaign against the Yue tribes">conquering the indigenous Baiyue</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[53]</a></sup> The region was consolidated under the <a href="/wiki/Nanyue" title="Nanyue">Nanyue</a> kingdom (a predecessor state of Vietnam) after the Qin collapse<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KeatGinOoi_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-KeatGinOoi-60">[54]</a></sup> and recaptured by China after the <a href="/wiki/Han_conquest_of_Nanyue" title="Han conquest of Nanyue">Han conquest</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[55]</a></sup> During the <a href="/wiki/Mongol_conquest_of_the_Song_dynasty" title="Mongol conquest of the Song dynasty">Mongol conquest of China</a> in the 13th century, the <a href="/wiki/Song_dynasty" title="Song dynasty">Southern Song</a> court was briefly located in modern-day <a href="/wiki/Kowloon_City" title="Kowloon City">Kowloon City</a> (the <a href="/wiki/Sung_Wong_Toi" title="Sung Wong Toi">Sung Wong Toi</a> site) before its final defeat in the 1279 <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Yamen" title="Battle of Yamen">Battle of Yamen</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Barber_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-Barber-62">[56]</a></sup> By the end of the <a href="/wiki/Yuan_dynasty" title="Yuan dynasty">Yuan dynasty</a>, seven large families had settled in the region and owned most of the land. Settlers from nearby provinces migrated to Kowloon throughout the <a href="/wiki/Ming_dynasty" title="Ming dynasty">Ming dynasty</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[57]</a></sup>
</p><p>The earliest European visitor was <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Portugal" title="Kingdom of Portugal">Portuguese</a> explorer <a href="/wiki/Jorge_%C3%81lvares" title="Jorge Álvares">Jorge Álvares</a>, who arrived in 1513.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[58]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[59]</a></sup> Portuguese merchants established a trading post called <a href="/wiki/Tam%C3%A3o" title="Tamão">Tamão</a> in Hong Kong waters and began regular trade with southern China. Although the traders were expelled after <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Tunmen" title="Battle of Tunmen">military clashes</a> in the 1520s,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[60]</a></sup> Portuguese-Chinese trade relations were <a href="/wiki/Luso-Chinese_agreement_(1554)" title="Luso-Chinese agreement (1554)">re-established by 1549</a>. Portugal acquired a permanent lease for <a href="/wiki/Macau" title="Macau">Macau</a> in 1557.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[61]</a></sup>
</p><p>After the <a href="/wiki/Transition_from_Ming_to_Qing" title="Transition from Ming to Qing">Qing conquest</a>, maritime trade was banned under the <i><a href="/wiki/Haijin" title="Haijin">Haijin</a></i> policies. From 1661 to 1683, the population of most of the area forming present day Hong Kong was cleared under the <a href="/wiki/Great_Clearance" title="Great Clearance">Great Clearance</a>, turning the region into a wasteland.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HK_story_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-HK_story-68">[62]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Kangxi_Emperor" title="Kangxi Emperor">Kangxi Emperor</a> lifted the maritime trade prohibition, allowing foreigners to enter Chinese ports in 1684.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[63]</a></sup> Qing authorities established the <a href="/wiki/Canton_System" title="Canton System">Canton System</a> in 1757 to regulate trade more strictly, restricting non-Russian ships to the port of <a href="/wiki/Guangzhou" title="Guangzhou">Canton</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[64]</a></sup> Although European demand for Chinese commodities like tea, silk, and porcelain was high, Chinese interest in European manufactured goods was insignificant, so that Chinese goods could only be bought with precious metals. To reduce the trade imbalance, the British sold large amounts of Indian <a href="/wiki/Opium" title="Opium">opium</a> to China. Faced with a drug crisis, Qing officials pursued ever more aggressive actions to halt the opium trade.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[65]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="British_colony">British colony</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/British_Hong_Kong" title="British Hong Kong">British Hong Kong</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hong_Kong_1868.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3255" data-file-width="4416" decoding="async" height="162" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Hong_Kong_1868.jpg/220px-Hong_Kong_1868.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Hong_Kong_1868.jpg/330px-Hong_Kong_1868.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Hong_Kong_1868.jpg/440px-Hong_Kong_1868.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hong_Kong_1868.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Hong Kong in 1868, photograph by <a href="/wiki/John_Thomson_(photographer)" title="John Thomson (photographer)">John Thomson</a></div></div></div>
<p>In 1839, the <a href="/wiki/Daoguang_Emperor" title="Daoguang Emperor">Daoguang Emperor</a> rejected proposals to legalise and tax opium and ordered imperial commissioner <a href="/wiki/Lin_Zexu" title="Lin Zexu">Lin Zexu</a> to eradicate the opium trade. The commissioner destroyed opium stockpiles and halted all foreign trade,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[66]</a></sup> triggering a British military response and the First Opium War. The Qing surrendered early in the war and ceded Hong Kong Island in the <a href="/wiki/Convention_of_Chuenpi" title="Convention of Chuenpi">Convention of Chuenpi</a>. British forces began controlling Hong Kong shortly after the signing of the convention, from 26 January 1841.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[67]</a></sup> However, both countries were dissatisfied and did not ratify the agreement.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[68]</a></sup> After more than a year of further hostilities, Hong Kong Island was formally ceded to the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_of_Great_Britain_and_Ireland" title="United Kingdom of Great Britain and Ireland">United Kingdom</a> in the 1842 <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Nanking" title="Treaty of Nanking">Treaty of Nanking</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Courtauld_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-Courtauld-75">[69]</a></sup>
</p><p>Administrative infrastructure was quickly built by early 1842, but piracy, disease, and hostile Qing policies initially prevented the government from attracting commerce. Conditions on the island improved during the <a href="/wiki/Taiping_Rebellion" title="Taiping Rebellion">Taiping Rebellion</a> in the 1850s, when many Chinese refugees, including wealthy merchants, fled mainland turbulence and settled in the colony.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CarrollEarlyHistory_23-2"><a href="#cite_note-CarrollEarlyHistory-23">[18]</a></sup> Further tensions between the British and Qing over the opium trade escalated into the Second Opium War. The Qing were again defeated and forced to give up <a href="/wiki/Kowloon_Peninsula" title="Kowloon Peninsula">Kowloon Peninsula</a> and <a href="/wiki/Stonecutters_Island" title="Stonecutters Island">Stonecutters Island</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Convention_of_Peking" title="Convention of Peking">Convention of Peking</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SecondOpiumWar_24-1"><a href="#cite_note-SecondOpiumWar-24">[19]</a></sup> By the end of this war, Hong Kong had evolved from a transient colonial outpost into a major <a href="/wiki/Entrep%C3%B4t" title="Entrepôt">entrepôt</a>. Rapid economic improvement during the 1850s attracted foreign investment, as potential stakeholders became more confident in Hong Kong's future.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[70]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Flag_of_Hong_Kong_(1959%E2%80%931997).svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="1200" decoding="async" height="110" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Flag_of_Hong_Kong_%281959%E2%80%931997%29.svg/220px-Flag_of_Hong_Kong_%281959%E2%80%931997%29.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Flag_of_Hong_Kong_%281959%E2%80%931997%29.svg/330px-Flag_of_Hong_Kong_%281959%E2%80%931997%29.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Flag_of_Hong_Kong_%281959%E2%80%931997%29.svg/440px-Flag_of_Hong_Kong_%281959%E2%80%931997%29.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Flag_of_Hong_Kong_(1959%E2%80%931997).svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/British_Hong_Kong" title="British Hong Kong">British Hong Kong</a> flag from 1959 to 1997</div></div></div>
<p>The colony was further expanded in 1898 when Britain obtained a 99-year lease of the New Territories.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NTLease_25-1"><a href="#cite_note-NTLease-25">[20]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/University_of_Hong_Kong" title="University of Hong Kong">University of Hong Kong</a> was established in 1911 as the territory's first institution of higher education.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[71]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Kai_Tak_Airport" title="Kai Tak Airport">Kai Tak Airport</a> began operation in 1924, and the colony avoided a prolonged economic downturn after the 1925–26 <a href="/wiki/Canton%E2%80%93Hong_Kong_strike" title="Canton–Hong Kong strike">Canton–Hong Kong strike</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KaiTak_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-KaiTak-78">[72]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[73]</a></sup> At the start of the <a href="/wiki/Second_Sino-Japanese_War" title="Second Sino-Japanese War">Second Sino-Japanese War</a> in 1937, Governor <a href="/wiki/Geoffry_Northcote" title="Geoffry Northcote">Geoffry Northcote</a> declared Hong Kong a neutral zone to safeguard its status as a free port.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[74]</a></sup> The colonial government prepared for a possible attack, evacuating all British women and children in 1940.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[75]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Imperial_Japanese_Army" title="Imperial Japanese Army">Imperial Japanese Army</a> <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Hong_Kong" title="Battle of Hong Kong">attacked Hong Kong on 8 December 1941</a>, the same morning as its <a href="/wiki/Attack_on_Pearl_Harbor" title="Attack on Pearl Harbor">attack on Pearl Harbor</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[76]</a></sup> Hong Kong was <a href="/wiki/Japanese_occupation_of_Hong_Kong" title="Japanese occupation of Hong Kong">occupied by Japan</a> for almost four years before Britain resumed control on 30 August 1945.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[77]</a></sup>
</p><p>Its population rebounded quickly after the war, as skilled Chinese migrants fled from the <a href="/wiki/Chinese_Civil_War" title="Chinese Civil War">Chinese Civil War</a> and more refugees crossed the border when the <a href="/wiki/Chinese_Communist_Party" title="Chinese Communist Party">Chinese Communist Party</a> took control of mainland China in 1949.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wiltshire_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wiltshire-84">[78]</a></sup> Hong Kong became the first of the <a href="/wiki/Four_Asian_Tigers" title="Four Asian Tigers">Four Asian Tiger</a> economies to industrialise during the 1950s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[79]</a></sup> With a rapidly increasing population, the colonial government attempted reforms to improve infrastructure and public services. The <a href="/wiki/Public_housing_in_Hong_Kong" title="Public housing in Hong Kong">public-housing estate programme</a>, <a href="/wiki/Independent_Commission_Against_Corruption_(Hong_Kong)" title="Independent Commission Against Corruption (Hong Kong)">Independent Commission Against Corruption</a>, and <a href="/wiki/MTR" title="MTR">Mass Transit Railway</a> were all established during the post-war decades to provide safer housing, integrity in the civil service, and more reliable transportation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[80]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[81]</a></sup>
</p><p>Nevertheless, widespread public discontent resulted in multiple protests from the 1950s to 1980s, including pro-<a href="/wiki/Republic_of_China_(1912%E2%80%931949)" title="Republic of China (1912–1949)">Republic of China</a> and pro-<a href="/wiki/Chinese_Communist_Party" title="Chinese Communist Party">Chinese Communist Party</a> protests. In the <a href="/wiki/1967_Hong_Kong_riots" title="1967 Hong Kong riots">1967 Hong Kong riots</a>, pro-<a href="/wiki/China" title="China">PRC</a> protestors clashed with the British colonial government. As many as 51 were killed and 802 were injured in the violence, including dozens killed by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Royal_Hong_Kong_Police" title="Royal Hong Kong Police">Royal Hong Kong Police</a> via beatings and shootings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[82]</a></sup>
</p><p>Although the territory's competitiveness in manufacturing gradually declined because of rising labour and property costs, it transitioned to a service-based economy. By the early 1990s, Hong Kong had established itself as a global financial centre and shipping hub.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[83]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Chinese_special_administrative_region">Chinese special administrative region</span></h3>
<p>The colony faced an uncertain future as the end of the New Territories lease approached, and <a href="/wiki/Murray_MacLehose,_Baron_MacLehose_of_Beoch" title="Murray MacLehose, Baron MacLehose of Beoch">Governor Murray MacLehose</a> raised the question of Hong Kong's status with <a href="/wiki/Deng_Xiaoping" title="Deng Xiaoping">Deng Xiaoping</a> in 1979.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[84]</a></sup> Diplomatic negotiations with China resulted in the 1984 <a href="/wiki/Sino-British_Joint_Declaration" title="Sino-British Joint Declaration">Sino-British Joint Declaration</a>, in which the United Kingdom agreed to transfer the colony in 1997 and China would guarantee Hong Kong's economic and political systems for 50 years after the transfer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carroll181_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-Carroll181-91">[85]</a></sup> The impending transfer triggered a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Waves_of_mass_migrations_from_Hong_Kong" title="Waves of mass migrations from Hong Kong">wave of mass emigration</a> as residents feared an erosion of civil rights, the rule of law, and quality of life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[86]</a></sup> Over half a million people left the territory during the peak migration period, from 1987 to 1996.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2002Pop_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-2002Pop-93">[87]</a></sup> The Legislative Council became a <a href="/wiki/1995_Hong_Kong_legislative_election" title="1995 Hong Kong legislative election">fully elected legislature</a> for the first time in 1995 and extensively expanded its functions and organisations throughout the last years of the colonial rule.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[88]</a></sup> Hong Kong was transferred to China on 1 July 1997, after 156 years of British rule.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYTHandover_27-1"><a href="#cite_note-NYTHandover-27">[22]</a></sup>
</p><p>Immediately after the transfer, Hong Kong was severely affected by several crises. The Hong Kong government was forced to use substantial <a href="/wiki/Foreign_exchange_reserves" title="Foreign exchange reserves">foreign exchange reserves</a> to maintain the Hong Kong dollar's currency peg during the <a href="/wiki/1997_Asian_financial_crisis" title="1997 Asian financial crisis">1997 Asian financial crisis</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wiltshire_84-1"><a href="#cite_note-Wiltshire-84">[78]</a></sup> and the recovery from this was muted by an <a href="/wiki/Influenza_A_virus_subtype_H5N1" title="Influenza A virus subtype H5N1">H5N1 avian-flu</a> outbreak<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[89]</a></sup> and a housing surplus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[90]</a></sup> This was followed by the <a href="/wiki/2002%E2%80%932004_SARS_outbreak" title="2002–2004 SARS outbreak">2003 SARS epidemic</a>, during which the territory experienced its most serious economic downturn.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[91]</a></sup>
</p><p>Political debates after the transfer of sovereignty have centred around the region's <a href="/wiki/Democratic_development_in_Hong_Kong" title="Democratic development in Hong Kong">democratic development</a> and the <a href="/wiki/State_Council_of_the_People%27s_Republic_of_China" title="State Council of the People's Republic of China">Chinese central government</a>'s adherence to the "one country, two systems" principle. After reversal of the last colonial era Legislative Council <a href="/wiki/1994_Hong_Kong_electoral_reform" title="1994 Hong Kong electoral reform">democratic reforms</a> following the handover,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[92]</a></sup> the regional government unsuccessfully attempted to enact <a href="/wiki/National_Security_(Legislative_Provisions)_Bill_2003" title="National Security (Legislative Provisions) Bill 2003">national security legislation</a> pursuant to <a href="/wiki/Hong_Kong_Basic_Law_Article_23" title="Hong Kong Basic Law Article 23">Article 23 of the Basic Law</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[93]</a></sup> The central government decision to implement <a href="/wiki/2014_NPCSC_Decision_on_Hong_Kong" title="2014 NPCSC Decision on Hong Kong">nominee pre-screening</a> before allowing <a href="/wiki/2014%E2%80%932015_Hong_Kong_electoral_reform" title="2014–2015 Hong Kong electoral reform">chief executive elections</a> triggered a series of <a href="/wiki/2014_Hong_Kong_protests" title="2014 Hong Kong protests">protests in 2014</a> which became known as the Umbrella Revolution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[94]</a></sup> Discrepancies in the electoral registry and disqualification of elected legislators after the <a href="/wiki/2016_Hong_Kong_legislative_election" title="2016 Hong Kong legislative election">2016 Legislative Council elections</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[95]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[96]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[97]</a></sup> and enforcement of national law in the <a href="/wiki/Hong_Kong_West_Kowloon_railway_station" title="Hong Kong West Kowloon railway station">West Kowloon high-speed railway station</a> raised further concerns about the region's autonomy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[98]</a></sup> In June 2019, <a href="/wiki/2019%E2%80%932020_Hong_Kong_protests" title="2019–2020 Hong Kong protests">mass protests erupted</a> in response to a <a href="/wiki/2019_Hong_Kong_extradition_bill" title="2019 Hong Kong extradition bill">proposed extradition amendment bill</a> permitting the extradition of fugitives to mainland China. The protests are the largest in Hong Kong history,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EconomistRestoreCalm_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-EconomistRestoreCalm-105">[99]</a></sup> with organisers claiming to have attracted more than three million Hong Kong residents.
</p><p>The Hong Kong regional government and Chinese central government responded to the protests with a number of administrative measures to quell dissent. In June 2020, the Legislative Council passed the <a href="/wiki/National_Anthem_Ordinance" title="National Anthem Ordinance">National Anthem Ordinance</a>, which criminalised "insults to the national anthem of China".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[100]</a></sup> The Chinese central government meanwhile enacted the <a href="/wiki/Hong_Kong_national_security_law" title="Hong Kong national security law">Hong Kong national security law</a> to help quell protests in the region.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cnn20200630_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-cnn20200630-107">[101]</a></sup> Nine months later, in March 2021, the Chinese central government introduced <a href="/wiki/2021_Hong_Kong_electoral_changes" title="2021 Hong Kong electoral changes">amendments to Hong Kong's electoral system</a>, which included the reduction of directly elected seats in the Legislative Council and the requirement that all candidates be vetted and approved by a Beijing-appointed <a href="/wiki/Candidate_Eligibility_Review_Committee" title="Candidate Eligibility Review Committee">Candidate Eligibility Review Committee</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-france2420210311_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-france2420210311-108">[102]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Government_and_politics">Government and politics</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Government_of_Hong_Kong" title="Government of Hong Kong">Government of Hong Kong</a>, <a href="/wiki/Politics_of_Hong_Kong" title="Politics of Hong Kong">Politics of Hong Kong</a>, <a href="/wiki/Elections_in_Hong_Kong" title="Elections in Hong Kong">Elections in Hong Kong</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Separation_of_powers_in_Hong_Kong" title="Separation of powers in Hong Kong">Separation of powers in Hong Kong</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Legislative_Council_Complex_2011_Chamber.JPG"><img alt="Large, round room with desks and a dais" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="1600" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/Legislative_Council_Complex_2011_Chamber.JPG/220px-Legislative_Council_Complex_2011_Chamber.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/Legislative_Council_Complex_2011_Chamber.JPG/330px-Legislative_Council_Complex_2011_Chamber.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/Legislative_Council_Complex_2011_Chamber.JPG/440px-Legislative_Council_Complex_2011_Chamber.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Legislative_Council_Complex_2011_Chamber.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Since 2012, the legislature has met in the <a href="/wiki/Tamar,_Hong_Kong" title="Tamar, Hong Kong">Tamar</a> <a href="/wiki/Legislative_Council_Complex" title="Legislative Council Complex">Legislative Council Complex</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Hong Kong is a <a href="/wiki/Special_administrative_regions_of_China" title="Special administrative regions of China">special administrative region of China</a>, with executive, legislative, and judicial powers <a href="/wiki/Devolution" title="Devolution">devolved</a> from the <a href="/wiki/Government_of_China" title="Government of China">national government</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[103]</a></sup> The Sino-British Joint Declaration provided for economic and administrative continuity through the transfer of sovereignty,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carroll181_91-1"><a href="#cite_note-Carroll181-91">[85]</a></sup> resulting in an <a href="/wiki/Executive_(government)" title="Executive (government)">executive-led</a> governing system largely inherited from the territory's history as a British colony.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BasicLaw15_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-BasicLaw15-110">[104]</a></sup> Under these terms and the "one country, two systems" principle, the <a href="/wiki/Hong_Kong_Basic_Law" title="Hong Kong Basic Law">Basic Law of Hong Kong</a> is the regional constitution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[105]</a></sup> The regional government is composed of three branches:
</p>
<ul><li><i>Executive:</i> The <a href="/wiki/Chief_Executive_of_Hong_Kong" title="Chief Executive of Hong Kong">Chief Executive</a> is responsible for enforcing regional law,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BasicLaw15_110-1"><a href="#cite_note-BasicLaw15-110">[104]</a></sup> can force reconsideration of legislation,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[106]</a></sup> and appoints <a href="/wiki/Executive_Council_of_Hong_Kong" title="Executive Council of Hong Kong">Executive Council</a> members and <a href="/wiki/Principal_officials_of_Hong_Kong" title="Principal officials of Hong Kong">principal officials</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[107]</a></sup> Acting with the Executive Council, the <a href="/wiki/King-in-Council#The_Commonwealth" title="King-in-Council">Chief Executive-in-Council</a> can propose new bills, issue <a href="/wiki/Primary_and_secondary_legislation" title="Primary and secondary legislation">subordinate legislation</a>, and has authority to <a href="/wiki/Dissolution_of_parliament" title="Dissolution of parliament">dissolve</a> the legislature.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[108]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/State_of_emergency" title="State of emergency">states of emergency</a> or public danger, the Chief Executive-in-Council is further empowered to enact any regulation necessary to restore public order.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[109]</a></sup></li>
<li><i>Legislature:</i> The unicameral <a href="/wiki/Legislative_Council_of_Hong_Kong" title="Legislative Council of Hong Kong">Legislative Council</a> enacts regional law, approves budgets, and has the power to <a href="/wiki/Impeachment" title="Impeachment">impeach</a> a sitting chief executive.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[110]</a></sup></li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Judiciary_of_Hong_Kong" title="Judiciary of Hong Kong">Judiciary</a>:</i> The <a href="/wiki/Court_of_Final_Appeal_(Hong_Kong)" title="Court of Final Appeal (Hong Kong)">Court of Final Appeal</a> and lower courts interpret laws and overturn those inconsistent with the Basic Law.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gittings150_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gittings150-117">[111]</a></sup> Judges are appointed by the chief executive on the advice of a recommendation commission.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[112]</a></sup></li></ul>
<p>The chief executive is the <a href="/wiki/Head_of_government" title="Head of government">head of government</a> and serves for a maximum of two five-year terms. The <a href="/wiki/State_Council_of_the_People%27s_Republic_of_China" title="State Council of the People's Republic of China">State Council</a> (led by the <a href="/wiki/Premier_of_the_People%27s_Republic_of_China" title="Premier of the People's Republic of China">Premier of China</a>) appoints the chief executive after nomination by the <a href="/wiki/Election_Committee_(Hong_Kong)" title="Election Committee (Hong Kong)">Election Committee</a>, which is composed of 1,200 business, community, and government leaders.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[113]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[114]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[115]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Legislative Council has 90 members, each serving a four-year term. Twenty are directly elected from <a href="/wiki/Geographical_constituency" title="Geographical constituency">geographical constituencies</a>, thirty-five represent <a href="/wiki/Functional_constituency_(Hong_Kong)" title="Functional constituency (Hong Kong)">functional constituencies</a> (FC), and forty are chosen by an <a href="/wiki/Election_Committee_(constituency)" title="Election Committee (constituency)">election committee</a> consisting of representatives appointed by the Chinese central government.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-overhaul_HK_122-0"><a href="#cite_note-overhaul_HK-122">[116]</a></sup> Thirty FC councillors are selected from limited electorates representing sectors of the economy or special interest groups,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LegCoVoting_123-0"><a href="#cite_note-LegCoVoting-123">[117]</a></sup> and the remaining five members are nominated from sitting <a href="/wiki/District_councils_of_Hong_Kong" title="District councils of Hong Kong">district council</a> members and selected in region-wide <a href="/wiki/Double_direct_election" title="Double direct election">double direct elections</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[118]</a></sup> All popularly elected members are chosen by <a href="/wiki/Proportional_representation" title="Proportional representation">proportional representation</a>. The 30 limited electorate functional constituencies fill their seats using <a href="/wiki/First-past-the-post_voting" title="First-past-the-post voting">first-past-the-post</a> or <a href="/wiki/Instant-runoff_voting" title="Instant-runoff voting">instant-runoff</a> voting.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LegCoVoting_123-1"><a href="#cite_note-LegCoVoting-123">[117]</a></sup>
</p><p>Twenty-two political parties had representatives elected to the Legislative Council in the <a href="/wiki/2016_Hong_Kong_legislative_election" title="2016 Hong Kong legislative election">2016 election</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[119]</a></sup> These parties have aligned themselves into three ideological groups: the <a href="/wiki/Pro-Beijing_camp_(Hong_Kong)" title="Pro-Beijing camp (Hong Kong)">pro-Beijing camp</a> (the current government), the <a href="/wiki/Pro-democracy_camp_(Hong_Kong)" title="Pro-democracy camp (Hong Kong)">pro-democracy camp</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Localism_in_Hong_Kong" title="Localism in Hong Kong">localist groups</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[120]</a></sup> The Chinese Communist Party does not have an official political presence in Hong Kong, and its members do not run in local elections.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[121]</a></sup> Hong Kong is represented in the <a href="/wiki/National_People%27s_Congress" title="National People's Congress">National People's Congress</a> by 36 deputies chosen through an electoral college and 203 delegates in the <a href="/wiki/Chinese_People%27s_Political_Consultative_Conference" title="Chinese People's Political Consultative Conference">Chinese People's Political Consultative Conference</a> appointed by the central government.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NationalReps_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-NationalReps-10">[8]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:HK_Chater_Road_LegCo_view.jpg"><img alt="The grey dome and front gable of a granite neo-classical building, with a skyscraper in the background against a clear blue sky" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="960" data-file-width="1280" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/HK_Chater_Road_LegCo_view.jpg/220px-HK_Chater_Road_LegCo_view.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/HK_Chater_Road_LegCo_view.jpg/330px-HK_Chater_Road_LegCo_view.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/HK_Chater_Road_LegCo_view.jpg/440px-HK_Chater_Road_LegCo_view.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:HK_Chater_Road_LegCo_view.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Court_of_Final_Appeal_Building" title="Court of Final Appeal Building">Court of Final Appeal Building</a> formerly housed the <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Court_(Hong_Kong)" title="Supreme Court (Hong Kong)">Supreme Court</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Legislative_Council_of_Hong_Kong" title="Legislative Council of Hong Kong">Legislative Council</a>.</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Law_of_the_People%27s_Republic_of_China" title="Law of the People's Republic of China">Chinese national law</a> does not generally apply in the region, and Hong Kong is treated as a separate jurisdiction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gittings150_117-1"><a href="#cite_note-Gittings150-117">[111]</a></sup> Its judicial system is based on <a href="/wiki/Common_law" title="Common law">common law</a>, continuing the legal tradition established during British rule.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[122]</a></sup> Local courts may refer to precedents set in <a href="/wiki/English_law" title="English law">English law</a> and overseas jurisprudence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[123]</a></sup> However, <a href="/wiki/Criminal_Procedure_Law_of_the_People%27s_Republic_of_China" title="Criminal Procedure Law of the People's Republic of China">mainland criminal procedure law</a> applies to cases investigated by the <a href="/wiki/Office_for_Safeguarding_National_Security_of_the_CPG_in_the_HKSAR" title="Office for Safeguarding National Security of the CPG in the HKSAR">Office for Safeguarding National Security of the CPG in the HKSAR</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NPC_Observer_130-0"><a href="#cite_note-NPC_Observer-130">[124]</a></sup> Interpretative and amending power over the Basic Law and jurisdiction over acts of state lie with the central authority, making regional courts ultimately subordinate to the mainland's <a href="/wiki/Socialist_law" title="Socialist law">socialist</a> <a href="/wiki/Civil_law_(legal_system)" title="Civil law (legal system)">civil law</a> system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[125]</a></sup> Decisions made by the <a href="/wiki/Standing_Committee_of_the_National_People%27s_Congress" title="Standing Committee of the National People's Congress">Standing Committee of the National People's Congress</a> override any territorial judicial process.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HCAL185/2016_132-0"><a href="#cite_note-HCAL185/2016-132">[126]</a></sup> Furthermore, in circumstances where the Standing Committee declares a state of emergency in Hong Kong, the State Council may enforce national law in the region.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BasicLawA18_133-0"><a href="#cite_note-BasicLawA18-133">[127]</a></sup>
</p><p>The territory's jurisdictional independence is most apparent in its <a href="/wiki/Visa_policy_of_Hong_Kong" title="Visa policy of Hong Kong">immigration</a> and taxation policies. The <a href="/wiki/Immigration_Department_(Hong_Kong)" title="Immigration Department (Hong Kong)">Immigration Department</a> issues <a href="/wiki/Hong_Kong_Special_Administrative_Region_passport" title="Hong Kong Special Administrative Region passport">passports for permanent residents</a> which differ from those of the mainland or Macau,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[128]</a></sup> and the region maintains a <a href="/wiki/Boundaries_of_Hong_Kong" title="Boundaries of Hong Kong">regulated border</a> with the rest of the country. All travellers between Hong Kong and China and Macau must pass through border controls, regardless of nationality.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[129]</a></sup> Mainland Chinese citizens do not have <a href="/wiki/Right_of_abode_in_Hong_Kong" title="Right of abode in Hong Kong">right of abode in Hong Kong</a> and are subject to immigration controls.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[130]</a></sup> Public finances are handled separately from the national government; taxes levied in Hong Kong do not fund the central authority.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[131]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[132]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Hong_Kong_Garrison" title="Hong Kong Garrison">Hong Kong Garrison</a> of the <a href="/wiki/People%27s_Liberation_Army" title="People's Liberation Army">People's Liberation Army</a> is responsible for the region's defence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[133]</a></sup> Although the <a href="/wiki/Chairman_of_the_Central_Military_Commission_(China)" title="Chairman of the Central Military Commission (China)">Chairman of the Central Military Commission</a> is <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Military_Command_of_the_People%27s_Republic_of_China" title="Supreme Military Command of the People's Republic of China">supreme commander</a> of the armed forces,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[134]</a></sup> the regional government may request assistance from the garrison.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[135]</a></sup> Hong Kong residents are not required to perform military service, and current law has no provision for local enlistment, so its defence is composed entirely of non-Hongkongers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[136]</a></sup>
</p><p>The central government and <a href="/wiki/Ministry_of_Foreign_Affairs_of_the_People%27s_Republic_of_China" title="Ministry of Foreign Affairs of the People's Republic of China">Ministry of Foreign Affairs</a> handle diplomatic matters, but Hong Kong retains the ability to maintain separate economic and cultural <a href="/wiki/Foreign_relations_of_Hong_Kong" title="Foreign relations of Hong Kong">relations with foreign nations</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[137]</a></sup> The territory actively participates in the <a href="/wiki/World_Trade_Organization" title="World Trade Organization">World Trade Organization</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Asia-Pacific_Economic_Cooperation" title="Asia-Pacific Economic Cooperation">Asia-Pacific Economic Cooperation</a> forum, the <a href="/wiki/International_Olympic_Committee" title="International Olympic Committee">International Olympic Committee</a>, and many <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a> agencies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[138]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[139]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[140]</a></sup> The regional government maintains <a href="/wiki/Hong_Kong_Economic_and_Trade_Office" title="Hong Kong Economic and Trade Office">trade offices</a> in <a href="/wiki/Greater_China" title="Greater China">Greater China</a> and other nations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[141]</a></sup>
</p><p>The imposition of <a href="/wiki/Hong_Kong_national_security_law" title="Hong Kong national security law">Hong Kong national security law</a> by the <a href="/wiki/National_People%27s_Congress_decision_on_Hong_Kong_national_security_legislation" title="National People's Congress decision on Hong Kong national security legislation">central government in Beijing</a> in June 2020 resulted in the suspension of bilateral extradition treaties by the United Kingdom, Canada, Australia, New Zealand, Finland, and Ireland.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rgmsus_148-0"><a href="#cite_note-rgmsus-148">[142]</a></sup> The United States ended its preferential economic and trade treatment of Hong Kong in July 2020 because it was no longer able to distinguish Hong Kong as a separate entity from the People's Republic of China.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rgmsus_148-1"><a href="#cite_note-rgmsus-148">[142]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bbcpref_149-0"><a href="#cite_note-bbcpref-149">[143]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Administrative_divisions">Administrative divisions</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/List_of_towns_in_Hong_Kong" title="List of towns in Hong Kong">List of towns in Hong Kong</a> and <a href="/wiki/Districts_of_Hong_Kong" title="Districts of Hong Kong">Districts of Hong Kong</a></div>
<p>The territory is divided into 18 districts, each represented by a <a href="/wiki/District_councils_of_Hong_Kong" title="District councils of Hong Kong">district council</a>. These advise the government on local issues such as public facility provisioning, community programme maintenance, cultural promotion, and environmental policy. There are a total of 479 district council seats, 452 of which are directly elected.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-district_150-0"><a href="#cite_note-district-150">[144]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Rural_committee" title="Rural committee">Rural committee</a> chairmen, representing outlying villages and towns, fill the 27 non-elected seats.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[145]</a></sup>
</p>
